Maturidi Proofs for Takwin's Eternity and Its Names
Maturidi reasoning for takwin's eternity and its multiple names.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi proof for the eternity of the attribute of takwin (creation) and how its name varies according to its effects.

Core Idea
Takwin is an eternal attribute of God, not the created thing itself.

The Maturidis argue that God's attribute of bringing things into existence (takwin) must be eternal, just as God's knowledge is eternal. This lesson presents their proof based on consensus and the impossibility of accidents in God, and then shows how this single attribute takes different names depending on what it produces.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation together. Notice the logical flow: first the distinction, then the proof, then the names.
Pay attention to the proof structure: consensus + analogy.
2
Analyze the main proof
Identify the two parts of the proof: (1) consensus that God creates, which requires an attribute of creation, and (2) that this attribute must be eternal because if it were temporal it would subsist in God, which is impossible.
The analogy 'like a knower without knowledge' is key.
3
Understand the naming principle
Notice how the same attribute (takwin) is given different names based on its effects: creation, provision, forming, life, death. This shows its unity despite diverse manifestations.
The text says 'according to the differences of effects'.
4
Consider the omitted point
Reflect on why the Maturidis 'remained silent' about the distinction between takwin and power. The commentary hints this is a contested point with the Ash'aris.
This silence is a clue for deeper debate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكونه غير الكون. وإن أزليته لا تستلزم أزلية المكونات، إلا أنهم سكتوا عما هو أصل الباب أعني مغايرته للقدرة) تختلف من حيث تعلقها بأحد طرفي الفعل والترك، واقترانها بإرادته، والعمدة في إثباته أن الباري تعالى مكون الأشياء إجماعاً وهو بدون صفة التكوين محال، كالعالم بلا علم، ولا بد أن تكون أزلية لامتناع قيام الحوادث بذات الله تعالى، ثم أسماؤها، بحسب اختلاف الآثار فمن حيث حصول المخلوقات به تسمى تخليقاً، والأرزاق ترزيقاً والصور تصويراً والحياة إحياء والموت إماتة إلى غير ذلك .
And that it is not the same as the created thing. And that its eternity does not entail the eternity of the created things. Except that they remained silent about what is the main point of the chapter, namely its distinction from power. It varies in terms of its relation to one of the two sides of action and omission, and its conjunction with His will. And the basis for its proof is that the Exalted God is the creator of things by consensus, and without the attribute of takwin it is impossible, like a knower without knowledge. And it must be eternal because of the impossibility of accidents subsisting in God's essence. Then its names vary according to the differences of effects: from the perspective of the occurrence of created things by it, it is called creation (takhlīq), provision (tarzīq), forming (taswīr), giving life (iḥyā'), causing death (imāta), etc.
Source pages p. 170

Commentary
This passage continues the Maturidi argument. They claim that takwin is not identical to the created thing (al-kawn), and its eternity does not imply eternal creation. However, the author notes that they omitted discussing the core issue: how takwin differs from power. The Maturidis then present their main proof: by consensus, God is the creator of things, and being a creator without an attribute of creation is impossible—just as a knower without knowledge is impossible. Thus, takwin must be an attribute. Furthermore, it must be eternal because if it were temporal (hādith), it would subsist in God, which is impossible (as accidents cannot subsist in God). Finally, they list various names for takwin based on different effects: when it brings about created things, it is called takhlīq (creation), when it provides sustenance, tarzīq, etc. This shows that takwin is a single attribute with multiple manifestations.

أزلية المكونات

تخليق

تصوير
إحياء
إماتة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that if takwin is eternal, then the created things must also be eternal.
Correction: The passage explicitly states: 'its eternity does not entail the eternity of the created things'. Takwin is eternal, but the products of takwin are temporal.

Key Takeaway
The Maturidis establish takwin as an eternal attribute of God distinct from the created world, proven by consensus and the impossibility of accidents in God, and named according to diverse effects.

Creation is Relational, Not a Real Attribute
Ash'ari answer: creation is relational, only requires power and will

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari argument that creation (takwin) is a mere relation between agent and effect, not a distinct eternal attribute, and therefore requires only power and will.

Core Idea
Creation is a relation, not a thing—so no extra eternal attribute needed.

The Maturidis argued that since a creator must have an attribute of creation (takwin), just as a knower has knowledge, takwin must be eternal. The Ash'ari author now responds by distinguishing real attributes from relational concepts. This passage shows why power and will are sufficient for creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the key terms: 'real attributes' (al-ṣifāt al-ḥaqīqiyya) and 'relation' (iḍāfa).
The passage contrasts two kinds of concepts: intrinsic attributes vs. relational meanings.
2
Identify the Ash'ari distinction
The author accepts the Maturidi analogy only for real attributes like knowledge and power, but denies it applies to 'effect' and 'bringing into existence'. Why? Because these are not intrinsic perfections but relations.
A relation (iḍāfa) exists only when its terms exist—creation exists when the creature exists.
3
Understand the consequence
If creation is a relation, it is temporal (exists only in the future, lā yazāl) and does not require an extra eternal attribute. Power and will already suffice to produce the effect.
This refutes the need for takwin as an eternal attribute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأجيب بأن ذلك إنما يكون هو في الصفات الحقيقية كالعلم والقدرة . ولا نسلم أن التأثير والإيجاد كذلك، بل هو معنى يعقل من إضافة المؤثر إلى الأثر، فلا يكون إلا فيما لا يزال، ولا يفتقر إلا إلى صفة القدرة والإرادة .
And it was answered that this (the analogy) only applies to real attributes like knowledge and power. And we do not grant that effect and bringing into existence are like that. Rather, it is a meaning understood from the relation of the agent to the effect, so it only exists in the future (lā yazāl), and it does not need anything except the attributes of power and will.
Source pages p. 170

Commentary
This is the Ash'ari response to the Maturidi proof for takwin's eternity. The Maturidis argued that just as a knower requires knowledge, a creator requires an attribute of creation. The Ash'ari author responds that this analogy holds only for real attributes (sifāt ḥaqīqiyya) like knowledge and power, which are intrinsic perfections. But 'effect' (ta'thīr) and 'bringing into existence' (ījād) are not real attributes; they are relational concepts understood from the connection between an agent and its effect. Such relations exist only when the effect exists (i.e., in time, 'lā yazāl' meaning in the future), and they do not require an additional eternal attribute—only the attributes of power and will, which are already sufficient for creation.

Key Terms
الصفات الحقيقية
التأثير
الإيجاد
إضافة المؤثر إلى الأثر
القدرة والإرادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that creation requires a separate attribute like knowledge does, so takwin must be eternal like knowledge.
Correction: The Ash'ari response shows that creation is a relation, not a real attribute. Relations are not eternal; they depend on the existence of their relata. Hence, no eternal attribute of creation is needed.

Checkpoint

A) The agent and the effect
B) Power and will
C) Knowledge and power
D) The eternal and the temporal

Key Takeaway
Creation is a relational meaning requiring only power and will, not a distinct eternal attribute.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Ash'aris teach that creation is not an eternal attribute added to God's essence but a relation that occurs when He wills. Does this make you feel more intimately connected to God, knowing that He is actively and directly involved in creating your reality each moment? The Argument from Divine Boasting in Eternal Speech
Maturidi proof: God's boasting in eternal speech proves takwin eternal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Maturidi argument from God's boasting in His eternal speech proves the eternity of the attribute of takwin.

Core Idea
God's eternal speech cannot contain false praise; therefore His attributes of creation are eternal.

This lesson presents an additional proof for the eternity of takwin, drawn from the nature of God's eternal speech. Since God praises Himself in the Quran as Creator, Maker, and Form-giver, these attributes cannot be temporal, otherwise His speech would contain falsehood. The argument is a concise logical demonstration that the attributes of creation must be co-eternal with God's essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the Proof
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice that it begins by stating there are other proofs, then presents one specific argument.
Focus on the logical flow: premise → impossibility → conclusion.
2
Identify the Premise
The premise is that God boasts in His eternal speech of being al-Khāliq, al-Bāri', al-Musawwir. Understand that this boasting is in the Quran, which is eternal (in Maturidi doctrine).
This premise is taken as certain.
3
Analyze the Two Impossibilities
The argument deduces two impossibilities if takwin were not eternal: (1) God would be boasting of what is not in Him (false praise), (2) He would acquire a perfection after lacking it (change in God). Both are impossible.
Trace how each impossibility flows from the premise.
4
Understand the Conclusion
Since both consequences are impossible, the original premise must be false—but it is true. Therefore the attributes of takwin must be eternal.
This is a reductio ad absurdum argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد يستدل بوجوه أخر. أحدها: أن الباري تعالى تمدح في كلامه الأزلي بأنه الخالق الباريء المصور فلو لم يثبت التخليق والتصور في الأزل، بل فيما لا يزال لكان ذلك تمدحاً من الله تعالى بما ليس فيه وهو محال ، ولزم اتصافه بصفة الكمال بعد خلوه عنها وهو عليه محال .
And other proofs are adduced. One of them: that the Exalted God boasts in His eternal speech that He is the Creator, the Maker, the Form-giver. So if creation and forming are not established in pre-eternity, but only in the future, then that would be God's boasting of what is not in Him, which is impossible, and it would necessitate His being described with an attribute of perfection after being devoid of it, which is also impossible.
Source pages p. 170

Commentary
This is an additional proof (likely from the Maturidi side) for the eternity of takwin. It argues that in God's eternal speech (the Quran), He praises Himself as the Creator (al-Khāliq), the Maker (al-Bāri'), the Form-giver (al-Muṣawwir). If these attributes were not true of God in pre-eternity but only later when creation occurs, then God's eternal speech would contain false praise—boasting of what He does not possess at the time of speech. This is impossible. Furthermore, it would imply that God becomes described with a perfection after lacking it, which implies change in God—also impossible. Therefore, the attributes of creation (takhlīq, taṣwīr) must be eternal.

Key Terms
التمدح
الكلام الأزلي
الخالق
الباريء
المصور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) God boasts in His eternal speech of being Creator
C) Creation occurs in time
D) The Quran is created

Key Takeaway
God's eternal praise of Himself as Creator necessitates that the attribute of creation is eternal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

When you recite 'Alhamdulillahi Rabbil 'Alameen', do you truly believe that His lordship and creativity are eternal and perfect right now? In what specific area of your life are you acting as if God's power to create change is limited or delayed?

?
The passage argues that God cannot acquire perfection after being devoid of it. This means He never lacks anything. Is there something you feel God 'lacks' in your life—like provision, help, or mercy—that you are waiting for Him to 'acquire'? First Argument: Quranic Praise and the Eternity of Takwin
First argument: Quranic praise implies eternal takwin, refuted.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Quranic verses praising God for creation do not prove that the attribute of takwin (bringing into existence) is eternal.

Core Idea
Praise for future acts does not demand eternal attributes.

The first argument attempts to prove takwin's eternity by citing Quranic verses that praise God for creating. Al-Taftazani presents this implicitly and then gives the Ash'ari response. The key is understanding that such praise applies to future acts, not to an eternal state.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument and its Quranic basis
Read the passage that cites verses like 'To Him glorify whatever is in the heavens...' as a potential proof for eternal takwin.
Note that the argument is implicit: praise for creation would be senseless if creation is not eternal.
2
Understand the Ash'ari refutation
The answer explains that the praise refers to actual deeds occurring in time, not in pre-eternity. Also, mentioning created things in pre-eternal speech does not imply their pre-eternity.
Example: the Quran mentions earth and heaven without implying they exist from eternity.
3
Reflect on the principle
Reflect on how divine attributes relate to future acts: God is eternally such that He will bring about creation, but that does not make the act itself eternal.
This introduces the concept of 'ḥuṣūl al-taʿalluqāt' (occurrence of relations) in time.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأجيب بأنه كالتمدح بقوله تعالى : يسبح له ما في السموات وما في الأرض (1) وقوله تعالى وهو الذي في السماء إله وفي الأرض إله (2) أي معبود، ولا شك أن ذلك بالفعل إنما يكون فيما لا يزال لا في الأزل والإخبار عن الشيء في الأزل لا يقتضي ثبوته فيه كذكر الأرض والسماء والأنبياء وغير ذلك. نعم هو في الأزل بحيث تحصل له هذه التعلقات والإضافات فيما لا يزال لما له (3) من صفات الكمال. وثانيها: أن الأشاعرة يقولون في قوله تعالى إنما قولنا لشيء إذا أردناه أن نقول له كن فيكون (4) أنه قد جرت العادة الإلهية أن تكون الأشياء لأوقاتها بكلمة أزلية كلمة (كن) ولا نعني بصفة التكوين إلا هذا. وأجيب : بأنه حينئذ يعود إلى صفة الكلام ولا تثبت صفة أخرى على أن الأكثر يجعلونه مجازاً عن سرعة الإيجاد والتكوين لما له من كمال العلم والقدرة والإرادة. وثالثها: أن التكوين والإيجاد صفة كمال، فلو خلا عنها في الأزل لكان نقصاً وهو عليه محال . وأجيب بأن ذلك إنما هو فيما يصح اتصافه به في الأزل. ولا نسلم أن التكوين والإيجاد بالفعل كذلك. نعم هو في الأزل قادر عليه ولا كلام فيه.
And it was answered: it is like praise in His statement, the Most High: 'To Him glorify whatever is in the heavens and whatever is in the earth' (1) and His statement, the Most High: 'And He is the one who in the heaven is God and in the earth is God' (2) meaning worshipped, and there is no doubt that that is by actual deed only in what is yet to come, not in pre-eternity, and informing about a thing in pre-eternity does not necessitate its being in it, like the mention of earth, heaven, prophets, etc. Indeed, He in pre-eternity is such that these relations and additions occur for Him in what is yet to come, due to His having (3) attributes of perfection. And the second: that the Ash'aris say regarding His statement, the Most High: 'Our saying to a thing when we will it is that we say to it "Be" and it is' (4) that the divine custom has been that things come into being at their times by an eternal word, the word 'Be', and we mean by the attribute of bringing into existence (takwin) nothing other than this. And it was answered: that then it reverts to the attribute of speech and no other attribute is established; moreover, most make it a metaphor for the swiftness of production and bringing into existence due to His perfection of knowledge, power, and will. And the third: that bringing into existence and production are an attribute of perfection, so if He were devoid of it in pre-eternity, that would be an imperfection, and that is impossible for Him. And it was answered: that this only applies to that which it is valid for Him to be qualified with in pre-eternity. And we do not grant that bringing into existence and production by actual deed are like that. Indeed, He in pre-eternity is able to do it, and there is no dispute about it.
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This passage presents three arguments used to prove that the attribute of bringing into existence (takwin) is eternal, along with the Ash'ari answers to each. The first argument (implicit) is that Quranic verses praising God for creation would require the creative act to be eternal. The answer clarifies that such praise refers to future acts, not pre-eternal ones—just as the Quran mentions created things without implying their pre-eternity. The second argument from the Ash'aris claims that the command 'Be' (كن) is an eternal word, and takwin is simply that. The answer reduces this to the attribute of speech, not a separate attribute, and notes that most interpret the verse metaphorically. The third argument states that takwin is a perfection, so its absence in pre-eternity would be a defect. The answer restricts perfection to what can be attributed in pre-eternity; actual creation is not an eternal state. The passage concludes by affirming that God is eternally capable of creation, but not actually creating from eternity.

Key Terms
التكوين
الأزل
التمدح
الإضافة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Quranic verses praising God for creation prove that the act of creation (takwin) is an eternal attribute.
Correction: The praise refers to actual deeds which occur in time; the Quranic mention of created things in pre-eternal speech does not imply their pre-eternity.

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'ari refutation, why does the Quranic praise 'To Him glorify whatever is in the heavens' not prove that takwin is eternal?
A) Because the praise is for actual deeds that occur in time, not in pre-eternity.
B) Because the Quranic verses are metaphorical, not literal.
C) Because God is not really praised for creation.
D) Because takwin is not about creation at all.

Key Takeaway
Quranic praise for creation refers to future acts, not to an eternal attribute of takwin.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Have you ever felt that your future is uncertain, and therefore hesitated to praise Allah for something not yet realized? How does the distinction between praise for an actual deed and praise for a pre-eternal attribute reshape your confidence in His promises?
?
This lesson shows that mentioning future creation in pre-eternal speech does not imply its pre-existence. How does this affect your understanding of the relationship between Allah's knowledge of your future and your own free will? Are you living as if Allah's decree is already set in stone, or as if it unfolds in time?

Second Argument: The Eternal Word 'Be' and Takwin
Second argument: eternal 'Be' equals takwin, refuted.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari claim that takwin is identical to the eternal word 'Be' (kun) and why this reduces takwin to the attribute of speech.

Core Idea
The 'Be' argument conflates takwin with kalām.

The second argument comes from the Ash'aris themselves, who interpret the Quranic command 'Be' (kun) as an eternal word and equate takwin with that word. Al-Taftazani presents this view and then gives the refutation, showing that this reduces takwin to the attribute of speech.

Guided Path
1
Read the Ash'ari argument
Read the passage where it says that Ash'aris consider the word 'Be' as an eternal word and that takwin is simply that.
This is based on Quran 16:40: 'Our saying to a thing when we will it is that we say to it "Be" and it is.'
2
Examine the refutation
The answer states that if takwin is the eternal word, then it reverts to the attribute of speech (kalām), not a separate attribute. Also, most scholars interpret the verse metaphorically.
The refutation does not deny the verse's validity but reinterprets it.
3
Consider the implications
Reflect on why distinguishing between takwin and kalām matters in Ash'ari theology.
If takwin is just kalām, then it is not a distinct eternal attribute; this affects the understanding of God's creative power.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأجيب بأنه كالتمدح بقوله تعالى : يسبح له ما في السموات وما في الأرض (1) وقوله تعالى وهو الذي في السماء إله وفي الأرض إله (2) أي معبود، ولا شك أن ذلك بالفعل إنما يكون فيما لا يزال لا في الأزل والإخبار عن الشيء في الأزل لا يقتضي ثبوته فيه كذكر الأرض والسماء والأنبياء وغير ذلك. نعم هو في الأزل بحيث تحصل له هذه التعلقات والإضافات فيما لا يزال لما له (3) من صفات الكمال. وثانيها: أن الأشاعرة يقولون في قوله تعالى إنما قولنا لشيء إذا أردناه أن نقول له كن فيكون (4) أنه قد جرت العادة الإلهية أن تكون الأشياء لأوقاتها بكلمة أزلية كلمة (كن) ولا نعني بصفة التكوين إلا هذا. وأجيب : بأنه حينئذ يعود إلى صفة الكلام ولا تثبت صفة أخرى على أن الأكثر يجعلونه مجازاً عن سرعة الإيجاد والتكوين لما له من كمال العلم والقدرة والإرادة. وثالثها: أن التكوين والإيجاد صفة كمال، فلو خلا عنها في الأزل لكان نقصاً وهو عليه محال . وأجيب بأن ذلك إنما هو فيما يصح اتصافه به في الأزل. ولا نسلم أن التكوين والإيجاد بالفعل كذلك. نعم هو في الأزل قادر عليه ولا كلام فيه.
And it was answered: it is like praise in His statement, the Most High: 'To Him glorify whatever is in the heavens and whatever is in the earth' (1) and His statement, the Most High: 'And He is the one who in the heaven is God and in the earth is God' (2) meaning worshipped, and there is no doubt that that is by actual deed only in what is yet to come, not in pre-eternity, and informing about a thing in pre-eternity does not necessitate its being in it, like the mention of earth, heaven, prophets, etc. Indeed, He in pre-eternity is such that these relations and additions occur for Him in what is yet to come, due to His having (3) attributes of perfection. And the second: that the Ash'aris say regarding His statement, the Most High: 'Our saying to a thing when we will it is that we say to it "Be" and it is' (4) that the divine custom has been that things come into being at their times by an eternal word, the word 'Be', and we mean by the attribute of bringing into existence (takwin) nothing other than this. And it was answered: that then it reverts to the attribute of speech and no other attribute is established; moreover, most make it a metaphor for the swiftness of production and bringing into existence due to His perfection of knowledge, power, and will. And the third: that bringing into existence and production are an attribute of perfection, so if He were devoid of it in pre-eternity, that would be an imperfection, and that is impossible for Him. And it was answered: that this only applies to that which it is valid for Him to be qualified with in pre-eternity. And we do not grant that bringing into existence and production by actual deed are like that. Indeed, He in pre-eternity is able to do it, and there is no dispute about it.
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This passage presents three arguments used to prove that the attribute of bringing into existence (takwin) is eternal, along with the Ash'ari answers to each. The first argument (implicit) is that Quranic verses praising God for creation would require the creative act to be eternal. The answer clarifies that such praise refers to future acts, not pre-eternal ones—just as the Quran mentions created things without implying their pre-eternity. The second argument from the Ash'aris claims that the command 'Be' (كن) is an eternal word, and takwin is simply that. The answer reduces this to the attribute of speech, not a separate attribute, and notes that most interpret the verse metaphorically. The third argument states that takwin is a perfection, so its absence in pre-eternity would be a defect. The answer restricts perfection to what can be attributed in pre-eternity; actual creation is not an eternal state. The passage concludes by affirming that God is eternally capable of creation, but not actually creating from eternity.

Key Terms
التكوين
كلمة كن
صفة الكلام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Quranic command 'Be' (kun) proves that takwin is an eternal, distinct attribute of speech.
Correction: The Ash'ari argument equates takwin with the eternal word, but the refutation shows this just reverts to the attribute of speech, and most scholars see it as metaphorical.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of the refutation to the second argument?
A) The verse is inauthentic.
B) If takwin is the eternal word, it is not a separate attribute but part of speech.
C) The word 'Be' is not eternal.
D) Takwin is actually the attribute of power.

Key Takeaway
The argument that takwin is the eternal word 'Be' is refuted by reducing it to the attribute of speech.

What concrete change will you make today to align your heart with the simplicity of 'Be, and it is'?

Third Argument: Takwin as a Perfection and the Impossibility of Its Absence
Third argument: perfection demands eternal takwin, refuted.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that takwin must be eternal because it is a perfection, and why the Ash'ari refutation rejects this by restricting perfections to what can be attributed in pre-eternity.

Core Idea
A perfection must be attributable in pre-eternity, not just in time.

The third and final argument posits that takwin is an attribute of perfection, so God cannot have been devoid of it in pre-eternity, as that would imply imperfection. Al-Taftazani presents the argument and then the Ash'ari answer, which draws a crucial distinction between being capable of an act and actually performing it in pre-eternity.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument from perfection
Read the passage that states that takwin is a perfection, so its absence in pre-eternity would be a defect, which is impossible for God.
This is a common line of reasoning in kalam: whatever is a perfection must be attributed to God eternally.
2
Study the Ash'ari refutation
The answer says that perfection only applies to what can be validly attributed in pre-eternity; actual bringing into existence (al-takwīn wa al-ījād bil-fiʿl) is not like that. God is eternally able to do it, but not actually doing it.
The key phrase: 'nām huwa fī l-azal qādir ʿalayhi wa lā kalām fīh' (Indeed, He in pre-eternity is able to do it, and there is no dispute about it).
3
Understand the distinction
Reflect on the difference between possessing a capacity (qudra) and actualizing that capacity. The Ash'ari view holds that God's attributes of perfection include power and will, but the act of creation is a temporal effect of those eternal attributes.
This preserves the eternity of essential attributes while allowing for temporal acts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And the second: that the Ash'aris say regarding His statement, the Most High: 'Our saying to a thing when we will it is that we say to it "Be" and it is' (4) that the divine custom has been that things come into being at their times by an eternal word, the word 'Be', and we mean by the attribute of bringing into existence (takwin) nothing other than this. Two Objections to Denying Eternal Takwin
Al-Taftazani presents two logical objections to the Ash'ari denial of an eternal takwin.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two logical objections raised against the Ash'ari denial of an eternal takwin and why they undermine that position.

Core Idea
Takwin is a relational concept, not an eternal attribute; eternal takwin would imply eternal effects.

In the previous discussions, the Ash'aris attempted to deny an eternal takwin by arguing that it would imply a change in God. Here, al-Taftazani presents two counter-objections that expose inconsistencies in that denial. The first reduces takwin to a mere relational concept, while the second shows that an eternal attribute of bringing into existence would necessitate eternal created things. Let us examine these objections carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage is structured as two numbered points.
2
Understand the first objection
The first objection argues that takwin is nothing but a relation (إضافة) between the agent and the effect. It is not an external entity.
This challenges the idea that takwin is a real eternal attribute.
3
Understand the second objection
The second objection uses a reductio: if takwin is eternal, then its effects (the things brought into existence) must also be eternal, which is impossible.
This shows a logical contradiction in affirming eternal takwin.
4
Reflect on the combined force
Consider how these two objections together undermine the Ash'ari position that takwin cannot be eternal. The first denies its reality as an attribute, the second denies its possibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم عورضت الوجوه المذكورة بوجهين : أحدهما : لا يعقل من التكوين إلا الإحداث وإخراج المعدوم من العدم إلى الوجود كما فسره القائلون بالتكوين الأزلي ولا خفاء في أنه إضافة يعتبرها العقل من نسبة المؤثر إلى الأثر فلا يكون عينياً موجوداً ثابتاً في الأزل. وثانيهما : أنه لو كان أزلياً لزم أزلية المكونات ضرورة امتناع التأثير بالفعل بدون الأثر.
Then the aforementioned arguments were countered with two points: One of them: nothing is understood from bringing into existence (takwin) except origination and bringing the non-existent out of non-existence into existence, as those who affirm eternal takwin have interpreted it, and it is no secret that it is a relation that the intellect considers as the connection of the agent to the effect, so it is not an external existent established in pre-eternity. And the second: that if it were eternal, then the created things would necessarily be eternal, due to the impossibility of actual influence without an effect.
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This passage provides two counter-objections to the previous Ash'ari arguments that denied an eternal takwin. The first objection argues that takwin, properly understood, is simply the act of bringing something from non-existence to existence—a relational concept, not an independent entity. Therefore, it cannot be an eternal attribute, as it depends on the existence of the effect. The second objection points out a logical consequence: if takwin were an eternal attribute, then the things it produces would also have to be eternal, because an actual cause cannot exist without its effect. This challenges the Ash'ari position that takwin is an eternal attribute that produces effects in time.

Key Terms
التكوين
إضافة
علاقة
أزلي
مكونات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that an eternal takwin is possible if it is a continuous attribute, but the second objection shows that continuity implies eternal effects.
Correction: The second objection demonstrates that an eternal attribute of bringing-into-existence would necessitate that the things brought into existence are also eternal, which is not the case. Hence, takwin cannot be eternal.

Checkpoint
What is the first counter-argument against the Ash'ari refutation of eternal takwin?
A) Takwin is a relation, not an eternal entity.
B) Takwin is identical to power.
C) The Quran proves takwin is eternal.
D) Takwin is a temporal accident.

Key Takeaway
The two objections show that the concept of an eternal takwin is either a mere relation or leads to eternal effects, both of which contradict the Ash'ari rejection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The second objection warns that an eternal attribute would force its effect to also be eternal. Is there anything in your life—a habit, a comfort, a relationship—that you treat as if it were eternal, something you cannot imagine losing? Will you let this objection unsettle that false security today?
?
If takwin is only a relation, not a thing 'out there' in Allah's essence, then what does that say about your attempts to confine Allah's action to your own mental categories? Are you willing to let go of controlling how God must work, and submit to the mystery of His creative will?

The Objector's Case for an Eternal Takwin
Objection presenting the definition and defense of eternal takwin.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objector's argument that takwin is an eternal attribute distinct from power and why he thinks this avoids making creation eternal.

Core Idea
Takwin's eternality doesn't make creatures eternal because it is directed at temporal moments.

The debate over takwin begins with an objection from those who posit a distinct eternal attribute of takwin. They argue that takwin's continuous direction at bringing things into existence at their appointed times allows it to be eternal without necessitating eternal creatures. This passage presents this objection and its rationale.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage where the objector defines takwin and explains why it is not power.
Notice the phrase 'فإن قيل' marking the objection.
2
Identify the key claims
Identify three claims: (1) Takwin is an eternal attribute for bringing things at their times, (2) It is not power because power only concerns possibility, (3) Eternal takwin does not entail eternal creatures because it is continuous until the moment of creation.
The objector uses the example of 'striking without something struck' to show a problem that only applies to transient accidents.
3
Reflect on the underlying issue
Ask yourself: What is the objector trying to preserve? The distinction between an eternal cause and a temporal effect is key.
This sets up for the Ash'ari response.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : المراد بالتكوين صفة أزلية بها تتكون الأشياء لأوقاتها، وتخرج من العدم إلى الوجود فيما لا يزال وليست نفس القدرة لأن مقتضى القدرة، ومتعلقها، إنما هو صحة المقدور وكونه ممكن الوجود ومقتضى التكوين ومتعقد وجود المكون في وقته على أنه لو أريد بالتكوين نفس الإحداث والإخراج من العدم، فأزليته لا تستلزم أزلية المخلوق، لأنه لما كان دائماً مستمراً إلى زمان وجود المخلوق، وترتبه عليه لم يكن هذا من انفكاك الأثر عن المؤثر، وتخلف المعلول عن العلة في شيء، ولم يكن كالضرب بلا مضروب والكسر بلا مكسور، وإنما يلزم ذلك في التكوين الذي يكون من الأعراض التي لا بقاء لها. قلنا : وما الدليل على أن تلك الصفة غير القدرة المتعلقة بأحد طرفي الفعل والترك المقترنة بإرادته. كيف وقد فسروا القدرة بأنها صفة تؤثر على وفق الإرادة أي إنما تؤثر بالفعل، ويجب صدور الأثر عنه عند انضمام الإرادة، وأما بالنظر إلى نفسها، وعدم اقترانها بالإرادة المرجحة لأحد طرفي الفعل والترك، فلا يكون إلا جائز التأثير، فلهذا لا يلزم وجود جميع المقدورات، ولما ذكرنا من أن القدرة جائز التأثير، وإنما يجب بالإرادة ، قال الإمام الرازي: إن الصفة التي تسمونها التكوين يكون تأثيرها أي بالنظر إلى نفسها إما على سبيل الجواز فلا تتميز عن القدرة أو على سبيل الوجوب فلا يكون الواجب مختاراً بل موجباً، ولا يرد عليه اعتراض صاحب التلخيص بأن الوجوب اللاحق لا ينافي الاختيار. لأن معناه أنه تعالى إذا أراد خلق شيء من مقدوراته ، كان حصول ذلك الشيء منه واجباً. لأن هذا هو القسم الأول. أعني ما يكون تأثيره بالنظر إلى نفسه على سبيل الجواز - قوله -
For if it is said: what is meant by bringing into existence is an eternal attribute by which things come into existence at their times and go out from non-existence into existence in what is yet to come, and it is not the same as power because the implication of power and its object is only the validity of the thing and its being possible of existence, while the implication of bringing into existence and the existence of the thing brought into existence at its time, and that if what is meant by bringing into existence is the very origination and bringing out from non-existence, then its eternality does not necessitate the eternality of the creature, because since it is continuous and lasting until the time of the creature's existence and its dependence upon it, this is not a case of the effect being separated from the agent or the consequent falling behind the cause, and it is not like striking without something struck or breaking without something broken; that only results in bringing into existence that is among accidents that have no permanence. We said: And what is the proof that that attribute is different from power which is related to one of the two sides of action and inaction and conjoined with His will? How, while they have interpreted power as an attribute that influences according to will, meaning only that it influences by action, and the effect must issue from it when will is joined, but by considering itself and its lack of conjunction with will that gives preponderance to one of the two sides of action and inaction, it is only possible of influence; therefore, it does not necessitate the existence of all things possible. And because we have mentioned that power is possible of influence, and it only becomes necessary by will, Imam al-Razi said: The attribute that you call bringing into existence, its influence, meaning by considering itself, is either on the basis of possibility, then it is not distinguished from power; or on the basis of necessity, then the Necessary Being would not be free (mukhtar) but rather necessitating (mujib), and the objection of the author of al-Talkhis that subsequent necessity does not contradict free will does not apply to this, because its meaning is that when He, the Most High, wills to create something from among His power-possibles, the occurrence of that thing from Him is necessary. Because this is the first division, that is, that whose influence by considering itself is on the basis of possibility - his statement -
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This passage presents an objection from the proponent of eternal takwin and the Ash'ari response, including a key argument by al-Razi. The objector defines takwin as an eternal attribute that brings things into existence at their times, distinct from power. He argues that eternal takwin does not entail eternal created things because it is continuous and directed at temporal effects. The answer (قلنا) challenges the claim that takwin is distinct from power, stating that power is also an attribute that influences according to will, and its influence is contingent unless joined by will. Al-Razi's argument provides a decisive dilemma: if takwin's influence in itself is merely possible, it is indistinguishable from power; if it is necessary, then God becomes a necessitating cause (mujib) rather than a free agent (mukhtar). The objection from the author of al-Talkhis that necessity after will is compatible with freedom is rebutted because the necessity in question here is inherent, not consequent on will.

Key Terms
التكوين
القدرة
الإحداث
الإخراج من العدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If takwin is eternal, then created things must also be eternal.
Correction: According to the objector, takwin's continuous relation to temporal effects does not require the effects to be coexistent.

Checkpoint
According to the objector, what is the object of power (qudra)?
A) Actual existence in time
B) Mere possibility of existence
C) Continuous creation
D) Necessary existence

Key Takeaway
The objector argues that takwin is an eternal attribute whose function is to bring about temporal existence without itself being temporal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The objector claims that God's attribute of bringing into existence (takwin) is eternal and continuously directed at you at your specific time of creation. Does this make you feel more or less significant in God's plan? How does it affect your sense of purpose? Commit to one action today that reflects the awareness that your existence is rooted in an eternal, intentional act of God.
?
If takwin is distinct from power (qudra), then God's power alone does not guarantee your actual existence—it required His eternal will to bring you forth at your appointed time. How does this distinction deepen your gratitude for being created? What can you do to live in a way that honors the specific moment God chose for your existence?

The Ash'ari Response: Power and Will
Ash'ari response denying the distinctness of takwin by explaining power's conditioned influence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari rebuttal that takwin is not distinct from power because power, when conjoined with will, suffices to bring about temporal effects.

Core Idea
Power's influence is conditional on will; it does not necessitate all effects.

In response to the objector, the Ash'ari theologian challenges the basis for distinguishing takwin from power. He argues that power, as defined, is an attribute that influences according to will, and its effect only becomes necessary when will is joined. Therefore, there is no need for a separate attribute of takwin.

Guided Path
1
Read the response
Read the Arabic passage beginning with 'قلنا' and note the logical structure: a challenge ('what is the proof?') followed by an explanation of power.
The response is in the first person plural ('قلنا') indicating the orthodox position.
2
Understand the definition of power
Notice that power is defined as an attribute that influences 'upon the pattern of will' (على وفق الإرادة). This means its influence is not automatic but determined by will.
This definition is crucial to refuting the need for takwin.
3
Connect to the broader argument
Reflect on how this explanation shows that power alone, with will, can account for specific acts of creation without needing a separate attribute.
The response also points out that if takwin were needed, it would still have the same issue of necessitation unless willed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : المراد بالتكوين صفة أزلية بها تتكون الأشياء لأوقاتها، وتخرج من العدم إلى الوجود فيما لا يزال وليست نفس القدرة لأن مقتضى القدرة، ومتعلقها، إنما هو صحة المقدور وكونه ممكن الوجود ومقتضى التكوين ومتعقد وجود المكون في وقته على أنه لو أريد بالتكوين نفس الإحداث والإخراج من العدم، فأزليته لا تستلزم أزلية المخلوق، لأنه لما كان دائماً مستمراً إلى زمان وجود المخلوق، وترتبه عليه لم يكن هذا من انفكاك الأثر عن المؤثر، وتخلف المعلول عن العلة في شيء، ولم يكن كالضرب بلا مضروب والكسر بلا مكسور، وإنما يلزم ذلك في التكوين الذي يكون من الأعراض التي لا بقاء لها. قلنا : وما الدليل على أن تلك الصفة غير القدرة المتعلقة بأحد طرفي الفعل والترك المقترنة بإرادته. كيف وقد فسروا القدرة بأنها صفة تؤثر على وفق الإرادة أي إنما تؤثر بالفعل، ويجب صدور الأثر عنه عند انضمام الإرادة، وأما بالنظر إلى نفسها، وعدم اقترانها بالإرادة المرجحة لأحد طرفي الفعل والترك، فلا يكون إلا جائز التأثير، فلهذا لا يلزم وجود جميع المقدورات، ولما ذكرنا من أن القدرة جائز التأثير، وإنما يجب بالإرادة ، قال الإمام الرازي: إن الصفة التي تسمونها التكوين يكون تأثيرها أي بالنظر إلى نفسها إما على سبيل الجواز فلا تتميز عن القدرة أو على سبيل الوجوب فلا يكون الواجب مختاراً بل موجباً، ولا يرد عليه اعتراض صاحب التلخيص بأن الوجوب اللاحق لا ينافي الاختيار. لأن معناه أنه تعالى إذا أراد خلق شيء من مقدوراته ، كان حصول ذلك الشيء منه واجباً. لأن هذا هو القسم الأول. أعني ما يكون تأثيره بالنظر إلى نفسه على سبيل الجواز - قوله -
For if it is said: what is meant by bringing into existence is an eternal attribute by which things come into existence at their times and go out from non-existence into existence in what is yet to come, and it is not the same as power because the implication of power and its object is only the validity of the thing and its being possible of existence, while the implication of bringing into existence and the existence of the thing brought into existence at its time, and that if what is meant by bringing into existence is the very origination and bringing out from non-existence, then its eternality does not necessitate the eternality of the creature, because since it is continuous and lasting until the time of the creature's existence and its dependence upon it, this is not a case of the effect being separated from the agent or the consequent falling behind the cause, and it is not like striking without something struck or breaking without something broken; that only results in bringing into existence that is among accidents that have no permanence. Al-Razi's Dilemma: Takwin Leads to Necessitarianism
Al-Razi's dilemma demonstrating the incoherence of a distinct takwin attribute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Razi's argument that an eternal takwin either collapses into power or forces God to be a necessitating cause, undermining divine freedom.

Core Idea
Eternal takwin either mimics power or makes God a necessary cause.

Imam al-Razi delivers a decisive dilemma: the attribute of takwin, if considered by itself, must have its influence either as possible or necessary. If possible, it is indistinguishable from power. If necessary, then God would not be free (mukhtar) but a necessitating agent (mujib). This refutes any attempt to posit takwin as a distinct attribute that preserves divine freedom.

Guided Path
1
Read al-Razi's dilemma
Read the Arabic passage where al-Razi presents the two options for takwin's influence.
Note the phrase 'إما على سبيل الجواز فلا تتميز عن القدرة أو على سبيل الوجوب فلا يكون الواجب مختاراً بل موجباً'.
2
Analyze each horn
First horn: if takwin's influence is possible (ja'iz), then it is no different from power. Second horn: if necessary, then God is a necessitating cause, contradicting His free choice.
The opponent of al-Talkhis might object that necessity after will is acceptable, but al-Razi counters that the necessity here is inherent, not consequent on will.
3
Understand the rebuttal to al-Talkhis
The passage clarifies that the necessity in question is not the subsequent necessity due to will, but the inherent necessity of the attribute itself.
This shows that any self-necessary influence eliminates divine freedom.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Checkpoint
According to al-Razi, what is the problem if takwin's self-influence is necessary?

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi's dilemma shows that any attempt to posit an eternal takwin distinct from power inevitably leads to either redundancy or necessitarianism, both of which are theologically problematic.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a trial, do you truly believe that God freely chooses to test you out of wisdom, or do you sometimes feel that your situation was forced upon you by a blind fate? How does al-Razi's insistence on God's freedom challenge you to see your trials as expressions of divine choice rather than impersonal necessity?
?
If you tend to attribute your successes to your own efforts and your failures to God's decree, how does this lesson expose that inconsistency? Al-Razi shows that God cannot be a necessitating cause—so how will you now take full responsibility for your choices while still trusting in God's wisdom?
?
In your moments of deepest gratitude, do you thank God as a personal, free agent who could have done otherwise, or do you simply acknowledge a law of cause and effect? This lesson forces you to choose: is your God a loving, selective Creator or a mechanical source? Which do your prayers reflect?

Al-Ash'ari's View on Influence and Effect
Al-Taftazani reports and dismisses al-Ash'ari's equation of influence with effect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Taftazani dismisses al-Ash'ari's equation of influence with effect as self-evidently absurd.

Core Idea
Al-Ash'ari's view that influence is identical to effect is dismissed as too absurd to need refutation.

In theological discussions of God's attributes, some schools debated whether God's attribute of takwin (bringing into existence) is identical to the thing brought into existence. Al-Ash'ari reportedly held that influence (ta'thir) is the very effect (athar). Al-Taftazani reports this view but immediately dismisses it as obviously corrupt. This lesson examines that dismissal and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice the brevity and the forceful rejection.
The passage ends with 'والذي يشعر به كلام' suggesting continuation.
2
Identify the reported view
Find the two equations: influence = effect, and takwin = mukawwan. Understand what each term means.
Influence (ta'thir) is the attribute, effect (athar) is the result.
3
Understand the dismissal
Al-Taftazani says the view is corrupt in its apparent meaning, and its corruption is so obvious it needs no proof. Discuss why this might be.
He uses 'fāsid' (corrupt) and 'ghaniyy 'an al-tanbīh' (beyond needing attention).
4
Consider the context
This is a sectarian discussion about divine attributes. Why might equating cause and effect be theologically problematic?
It could imply God's attribute is not distinct from creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما نقل قد اشتهر عن الأشعري أن التأثير نفس الأثر والتكوين نفس المكون ، وهذا بظاهره فاسد، وفساده غني عن التنبيه فضلاً عن الدليل والذي يشعر به كلام
And what has been transmitted, which is well-known from al-Ash'ari, that influence is the very effect and bringing into existence is the very thing brought into existence, and this in its apparent meaning is corrupt, and its corruption is beyond needing attention, let alone proof, and that which is indicated by the statement
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This brief passage reports a famous statement attributed to al-Ash'ari: that influence (ta'thir) is identical to the effect (athar), and takwin is identical to the thing brought into existence (mukawwan). The author dismisses this view as evidently false, so much so that it requires no proof; its corruption is obvious even without analysis. The phrase 'and that which is indicated by the statement' suggests the author is about to elaborate, but the passage ends here, possibly continuing on the next page.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think equating influence with effect is a subtle metaphysical point about divine attributes.
Correction: Al-Taftazani states it is patently false (fāsid) and requires no refutation because its corruption is obvious.

Checkpoint
How does al-Taftazani characterize al-Ash'ari's equation of influence with effect?
A) It is a subtle metaphysical insight.
B) It is evidently corrupt and needs no refutation.
C) It is correct but misinterpreted by others.
D) It has no relation to the discussion.

Key Takeaway
This passage shows the forceful dismissal of the view that influence is identical to effect as patently absurd without need for proof.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The author here is certain that equating influence with effect is absurd. Is there any belief you currently hold that you are equally certain about? How would you react if someone told you that your certainty itself is a sign of pride?
?
If God's attributes and their effects are truly distinct, what does that mean for how you relate to the blessings in your life? Do you see them as separate from the Giver, or do you conflate the two, forgetting the attribute behind the effect?

The Objection Against Eternal Takwin and the Response
Al-Taftazani refutes the objection of eternal creation from a takwin that is eternal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that an eternal takwin would entail eternal creation and how the response clarifies that takwin is an eternal attribute that brings about creation at its appointed time without temporal separation.

Core Idea
Eternal takwin does not require eternal creation; it is a continuous attribute.

In this passage, al-Taftazani presents a second objection to the eternity of takwin: if takwin is eternal, then created things must also be eternal. He then provides a nuanced response distinguishing between the attribute and the effect, arguing that takwin is perpetual until the moment of creation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Read the first sentence to find the objection that if takwin is eternal, then created things must be eternal. Note the reasoning: an effect cannot be separated from an effecter in action.
2
Understand the initial response
Then read the response: takwin is an eternal attribute that brings things into being at their times. Notice how the respondent distinguishes the attribute from the act.
3
Examine the stronger version of the objection
The objector then says: even if takwin is the act itself, its eternity still requires eternal effect. The author responds by explaining that takwin is continuous, not an accidental effect.
4
Contrast with accidental effects
Note the analogy: striking and breaking are accidental effects that cannot persist, but takwin is a permanent attribute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وثانيهما : أنه لو كان أزلياً لزم أزلية المكونات ضرورة امتناع التأثير بالفعل بدون أثر. فإن قيل : المراد بالتكوين صفة أزلية بها تتكون الأشياء لأوقاتها، وتخرج من العدم إلى الوجود فيما لا يزال وليست نفس القدرة لأن مقتضى القدرة، ومتعلقها، إنما هو صحة المقدور وكونه ممكن الوجود ومقتضى التكوين ومتعلق[؟] وجود المكون في وقته على أنه لو أريد بالتكوين نفس الإحداث والإخراج من العدم، فأزليته لا تستلزم أزلية المخلوق، لأنه لما كان دائماً مستمراً إلى زمان وجود المخلوق، وترتبه عليه لم يكن هذا من انفكاك الأثر عن المؤثر، وتخلف المعلول عن العلة في شيء، ولم يكن كالضرب بلا مضروب والكسر بلا مكسور، وإنما يلزم ذلك في التكوين الذي يكون من الأعراض التي لا بقاء لها . قلنا : وما الدليل على أن تلك الصفة غير القدرة المتعلقة بأحد طرفي الفعل والترك المقترنة بإرادته. كيف وقد فسروا القدرة بأنها صفة تؤثر على وفق الإرادة أي إنما تؤثر بالفعل، ويجب صدور الأثر عنه عند انضمام الإرادة، وأما بالنظر إلى نفسها، وعدم اقترانها بالإرادة المرجحة لأحد طرفي الفعل والترك، فلا يكون إلا جائز التأثير، فلهذا لا يلزم وجود جميع المقدورات، ولما ذكرنا من أن القدرة جائز التأثير، وإنما يجب بالإرادة ، قال الإمام الرازي: إن الصفة التي تسمونها التكوين يكون تأثيرها أي بالنظر إلى نفسها إما على سبيل الجواز فلا تتميز عن القدرة أو على سبيل الوجوب فلا يكون الواجب مختاراً بل موجباً، ولا يرد عليه اعتراض صاحب التلخيص بأن الوجوب اللاحق لا ينافي الاختيار. لأن معناه أنه تعالى إذا أراد خلق شيء من مقدوراته ، كان حصول ذلك الشيء منه واجباً. لأن هذا هو القسم الأول. أعني ما يكون تأثيره بالنظر إلى نفسه على سبيل الجواز.
And the second: that if it were eternal, the eternity of created things would necessarily follow by the impossibility of effecting an act without an effect. If it is said: What is meant by takwin is an eternal attribute by which things come into being at their times, and come out of non-existence into existence in what is yet to come, and it is not the same as power (qudra) because the requirement of power and its object is merely the validity of the determined thing (maqdur) and its being possible of existence, while the requirement of takwin and its object[?] is the existence of the determined thing at its time, and if takwin were meant to be the very act of bringing about and bringing out of non-existence, then its eternity does not entail the eternity of the created thing, because since it is perpetual and continuous up to the time of the existence of the created thing and its dependence on it, this is not a case of separation of effect from effecter, nor of the caused lagging behind the cause in any way, and it is not like striking without a struck object or breaking without a broken object. That only follows in the case of takwin that is among accidents that have no duration. We say: What is the proof that this attribute is other than power (qudra) which pertains to one of the two sides of action and omission and is conjoined with will? How so, when they have explained power as an attribute that effects in accordance with will, meaning it only effects by means of the act, and the effect must issue from it when will is joined, but with respect to itself and the absence of will that gives preponderance to one of the two sides of action and omission, it is only possible of effect. For this reason, the existence of all determined things is not necessary. And based on what we have mentioned, that power is possible of effect and only becomes necessary by will, Imam al-Razi said: The attribute that you call takwin—its effect, that is, with respect to itself—is either on the basis of possibility, in which case it is not distinguished from power, or on the basis of necessity, in which case the Necessary Being would not be free (mukhtar) but would be a necessitating cause (mujib). And the objection of the author of al-Talkhis that subsequent necessity does not negate free choice does not apply to him, because its meaning is that when He wills to create something from among His determined things, the occurrence of that thing from Him is necessary. This is the first division, namely that whose effect with respect to itself is on the basis of possibility.
Source pages p. 172

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer argument concerning the attribute of takwin (bringing into existence). The author presents a second objection: if takwin were eternal, then the created things would also be eternal, because an effect cannot be separated from its cause. The respondent (presumably the Ash'ari theologian) clarifies that takwin is an eternal attribute that brings things into existence at their appointed times, not in eternity. Thus, the created things are temporal, not eternal. The objector then argues that if takwin is the very act of bringing into existence, its eternity would still require the created thing to be eternal because the effect would be simultaneous with the cause. The respondent counters that takwin is a continuous attribute that persists until the time of creation; therefore, the effect follows the cause without temporal separation, unlike accidental effects like striking or breaking. The author then shifts to a different point: what proof is there that takwin is distinct from power (qudra)? He notes that power, according to their definition, only becomes effective when will is attached; by itself, it is merely possible. Therefore, power does not necessitate the existence of all possible things. Imam al-Razi then argues that if takwin's effect is considered in itself, it is either possible (like power, thus indistinguishable) or necessary (making God a necessitating cause, which violates free choice). The author rejects an objection from the author of al-Talkhis that subsequent necessity does not negate free choice, explaining that the necessity in question is the result of will, not inherent in the attribute itself, and thus belongs to the first division (possible effect).

Key Terms
التكوين
الأزل
المكونات
التأثير
الأثر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some argue that an eternal cause must have an eternal effect.
Correction: The author argues that if the cause is a continuous attribute that remains until the effect's time, the effect can be temporal.

Checkpoint
According to the response, why does eternal takwin not necessitate eternal creation?
A) Because takwin is not eternal
B) Because takwin is a continuous attribute that persists until the time of creation
C) Because creation happens in eternity
D) Because takwin is an accident

Key Takeaway
Takwin is an eternal attribute that brings things into existence at their appointed times without requiring the effect to be co-eternal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often demand immediate answers from God, but His takwin brings things at their appointed times. Is there a situation where your impatience reveals a lack of trust in His perpetual attribute of bringing things into existence exactly when He wills? What would it look like to surrender that timeline to Him today?
?
The passage insists that takwin is not like power—it doesn't just make things possible; it actually brings them into existence at the right moment. Do you live as if God is merely able to help you, or as if He is actively causing the right outcomes at the right times? Where have you settled for possibility instead of trusting His active bringing-forth?
?
The objection assumed that an eternal cause must have an eternal effect. Have you ever assumed that because God is eternal, His solutions should be instant and without delay? How does understanding that takwin is a continuous attribute—working through time to its appointed end—change the way you wait for Him to act in your life? Commit to one area where you will wait actively, trusting His takwin.

Distinguishing Takwin from Qudra: The Razi Argument
Al-Taftazani argues that takwin cannot be distinct from qudra based on Razi's dilemma.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that takwin cannot be a separate attribute from qudra because if its effect is possible, it is identical to qudra, and if necessary, it negates free choice.

Core Idea
Takwin either has possible effect (same as qudra) or necessary effect (violates divine freedom).

After refuting the objection about eternity, al-Taftazani turns to a more fundamental challenge: what proof is there that takwin is distinct from power (qudra)? He cites Imam al-Razi's argument that the effect of takwin, considered in itself, must be either possible or necessary. Neither option allows for a meaningful distinction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the challenge
Read the opening question: what evidence is there that takwin is distinct from qudra?
2
Understand the definition of qudra
Notice that qudra is defined as effecting only in accordance with will, so by itself it is merely possible.
3
Examine Razi's dilemma
Takwin's effect in itself must be either possible (like qudra) or necessary (making God a necessitating cause).
4
Consider the objection and response
The author addresses an objection that subsequent necessity does not negate free choice, but clarifies that Razi's necessary division refers to inherent necessity, not will-based necessity.

?

What would change if you truly believed that His freedom is perfect and His choice is always better than what you demand?

Al-Ash'ari on Takwin: Critique and Interpretations
Al-Taftazani critiques and interprets al-Ash'ari's view on takwin.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Ash'ari's apparent identification of takwin with the effect is considered corrupt, and how two interpretations (linguistic and philosophical) attempt to reconcile it.

Core Idea
Takwin and the effect are not identical; any identification is either linguistic or mental, not real.

In this passage, al-Taftazani examines a famous statement attributed to al-Ash'ari: that takwin (bringing into existence) is the same as the thing brought into existence. He finds the apparent meaning obviously corrupt, but then explores two possible interpretations from followers. This opens up a discussion on whether creation is a real attribute or merely a mental consideration.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the strong dismissal of the apparent meaning.
2
Identify the two interpretations
The passage presents two ways to understand al-Ash'ari's statement. Find each one and note how the author responds to them.
The first is linguistic, the second is philosophical.
3
Reflect on the author's conclusion
Ask yourself why the author dismisses the first interpretation as unsuitable for scientific investigations while finding the second plausible. How does the concept of 'mental consideration' (i'tibar 'aqli) resolve the issue?
This connects to broader discussions in ontology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما نقل قد اشتهر عن الأشعري أن التأثير نفس الأثر والتكوين نفس المكون ، وهذا بظاهره فاسد، وفساده غني عن التنبيه فضلاً عن الدليل والذي يشعر به كلام بعض الأصحاب أن معناه أن لفظ الخلق شائع في المخلوق بحيث لا يفهم منه عند الإطلاق غيره سواء جعلناه حقيقة فيه أو مجازاً اشتهر من الخلق بمعنى المصدر، وهذا لا يليق بالمباحث العلمية، ويمكن أن يكون معناه أن الشيء إذا أثر في شيء وأوجده بعد ما لم يكن مؤثراً ، فالذي حصل في الخارج هو الأثر لا غير، وأما حقيقة الإحداث والإيجاد فاعتبار عقلي لا تحقق له في الأعيان، وقد سبق ذلك في الأمور العامة .
And what has been transmitted and become famous from al-Ash'ari, that effect (ta'thir) is the same as the effect (athar) and takwin is the same as the constituted thing (mukawwan). This, in its apparent meaning, is corrupt, and its corruption is too obvious for notification, let alone proof. What the speech of some of the followers suggests is that its meaning is that the term 'creation' (khalq) is commonly used for the created thing (makhluq) so that nothing else is understood from it when used absolutely, whether we take it as a literal meaning or as a metaphor that has become prevalent for 'creation' in the sense of the verbal noun. This is not suitable for scientific investigations. And it is possible that its meaning is that when a thing effects something and brings it into existence after it was not an effecter, what occurs in the external world is the effect alone, nothing else. As for the reality of bringing about and bringing into existence, it is a mental consideration with no realization in external entities. This has already been discussed in al-Umur al-Amma.
Source pages p. 172

Commentary
This passage is a critique and interpretation of a famous statement attributed to al-Ash'ari: that 'effect (ta'thir) is the same as the effect (athar)' and 'takwin is the same as the constituted thing (mukawwan)'. The author declares that this apparent meaning is obviously corrupt and needs no proof of its falsity. He then presents two possible interpretations from some of al-Ash'ari's followers. The first interpretation is linguistic: the word 'khalq' (creation) is so commonly used to refer to the created thing (makhluq) that, when used without qualification, it is understood to mean the created thing, whether as a literal meaning or as a prevalent metaphor. The author dismisses this as unsuitable for scientific discourse. The second interpretation is philosophical: when something effects another thing and brings it into existence, what exists externally is only the effect (the created thing), while the act of bringing into existence (takwin) is a mental consideration (i'tibar 'aqli) with no external reality. This has been previously discussed in the section on general matters (al-Umur al-Amma).

Key Terms

الأثر
التكوين
المكون
الخلق
المخلوق
اعتبار عقلي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that al-Ash'ari literally identified takwin with the effect, meaning that the act of creating is identical to the created object in reality.
Correction: The author shows that the apparent meaning is obviously corrupt and that acceptable interpretations involve either linguistic usage or holding that the act of creation is a mental consideration, not an external entity.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is one possible interpretation of al-Ash'ari's statement?
A) That takwin is a distinct eternal attribute
B) That the term 'creation' is commonly used for the created thing
C) That takwin and effect are identical in reality
D) That God's power is the same as takwin

Key Takeaway
Al-Ash'ari's apparent identification of takwin with the effect is understood either as a linguistic convention or as pointing to the mental consideration of the act of bringing into existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about your own actions, do you habitually conflate the act with its result? How does this passage's insistence on distinguishing between the mental act of 'causing' and the external effect challenge you to examine your sense of responsibility before Allah? Will you now pause to differentiate between your intentions and their actual outcomes in your daily life?
?
The author dismisses a surface-level reading of al-Ash'ari as 'obviously corrupt'. Are you ever satisfied with a superficial understanding of theological issues without pressing for deeper coherence? How might that intellectual laziness affect the sincerity of your worship and your reliance on sound knowledge? What step will you take today to seek a deeper, more precise understanding of one aspect of your creed?
?
This passage argues that takwin is a mental consideration, not an external entity. When you pray or perform an act of worship, do you often focus on the outward action while neglecting the inward reality of your intention and the mental act of devotion? How can you ensure that your acts of worship are not merely external motions but are accompanied by a conscious mental consideration of their meaning?

Disputed Attributes: Eternity, Mercy, and Will
Author reports views on eternity, mercy, pleasure, and generosity as divine attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the disagreement over whether attributes like eternity, mercy, pleasure, and generosity are distinct from the essence or referred to the will.

Core Idea
The majority refer mercy and generosity to will; Ibn Sa'id sees them as separate.

The author begins by noting that among the disputed divine attributes are eternity, mercy, pleasure, and generosity. He reports two views: the majority holds that God is eternal by His essence and that these other attributes are referred back to the will, while Ibn Sa'id affirms them as distinct attributes beyond the will. This lesson examines these positions and the critique of Ibn Sa'id's lack of proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on disputed attributes
Read the first part of the source passage that covers eternity, mercy, pleasure, and generosity. Identify the two opposing views.
The passage contrasts the majority view with Ibn Sa'id's view.
2
Understand the majority position
The majority say God is eternal by His essence, not by an additional attribute. They also refer mercy, pleasure, and generosity to the attribute of will.
This is the more accepted Ash'ari position.
3
Examine Ibn Sa'id's view
Ibn Sa'id affirms eternity as a distinct attribute and also affirms mercy, pleasure, and generosity as attributes beyond will.
The author notes that Ibn Sa'id has no valid proof for his position.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this distinction matters: it affects how we understand God's attributes as either identical to essence or additional.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
من الصفات المختلف فيها قال : ومنها القدم والرحمة والرضا والكرم عند أبي سعيد والجمهور على أنه تعالى قديم بذاته ومرجع البواقي إلى الإرادة . قال : ومنها القدم أثبته ابن سعيد صفة يكون بها الباري قديماً، وأثبت الرحمة والكرم والرضا صفات وراء الإرادة وليس له على ذلك دليل يعود عليه . وأثبت القاضي إدراك الشم والذوق واللمس صفات وراء العلم .
From the disputed attributes: He said: Among them are Eternity, Mercy, Pleasure, and Generosity according to Abu Sa'id, and the majority hold that He is Eternal by His Essence and the rest are referred to Will. He said: Among them, Eternity: Ibn Sa'id affirmed it as an attribute by which the Creator is Eternal, and he affirmed Mercy, Generosity, and Pleasure as attributes beyond Will, and he has no proof that goes back to him. And al-Qadi affirmed the perception of smell, taste, and touch as attributes beyond Knowledge.
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This passage enumerates disputed divine attributes. The author first reports the view of Abu Sa'id and the majority: the attribute of Qidam (eternity) is not a separate attribute; rather, God is eternal by His essence, and other attributes like Mercy, Pleasure, and Generosity are referred to the Will (irāda). Then he mentions the opposing view of Ibn Sa'id, who affirms Qidam as a distinct attribute, and also affirms Mercy, Pleasure, and Generosity as attributes beyond Will, but notes that he has no valid proof. Finally, al-Qadi (al-Qāḍī ʿAbd al-Jabbār?) affirms that God has attributes of perception (idrāk) for smell, taste, and touch, which are beyond Knowledge.

Anthropomorphic Verses Are Metaphorical
Author argues that anthropomorphic terms are metaphors and states their specific theological equivalents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ash'ari tradition interprets Qur'anic anthropomorphic expressions as metaphors for divine attributes.

Core Idea
Anthropomorphic verses are metaphors for divine majesty, power, existence, and sight.

This passage addresses a key issue in Islamic theology: how to understand verses that seem to ascribe human bodily parts to Allah. The author argues that such terms cannot be taken literally and are instead metaphors or representations that safeguard divine transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its English translation. Notice the four key terms: istiwa', yad, wajh, and 'ayn.
The passage also includes a reference to al-Ash'ari's two opinions.
2
Understand the impossibility of literalism
The author states that it is impossible (mumtani') to carry these terms on their real (literal) meanings. Reflect on why this is the case for preserving divine transcendence.
This is a foundational principle in tanzih.
3
Identify the metaphorical equivalents
The passage gives specific metaphorical interpretations for each term. List them: istiwa' = domination/depiction, yad = power, wajh = existence, 'ayn = sight.
These interpretations are from the majority view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : ومنها ما ورد . كالاستواء واليد والوجه والعين ونحو ذلك والحق أنها مجازات وتمثيلات. قال : ومنها ما ورد به ظاهر الشرع، وامتنع حملها على معانيها الحقيقية مثل الاستواء في قوله تعالى الرحمن على العرش استوى ) (۱) واليد في قوله تعالى يد الله فوق أيديهم ) (۲) ما منعك أن تسجد لما خلقت بيدي (٣) والوجه في قوله تعالى ويبقى وجه ربك (٤) والعين في قوله تعالى ولتصنع على عيني (0) تجر بأعيننا) (1) فعن الشيخ أن كلا منها صفة زائدة . وعند الجمهور : وهو أحد قولي الشيخ أنها مجازات فالاستواء مجاز عن الاستيلاء أو تمثيل وتصوير لعظمة الله تعالى واليد مجاز عن القدرة والوجه عن الوجود والعين عن البصر .
He said: Among them are what have been reported, like al-istiwa, al-yad, al-wajh, al-ayn, and the like; and the truth is that they are metaphors and representations. He said: Among them are what have been reported by the apparent text of the Law, and it is impossible to carry them upon their real meanings, such as al-istiwa in His statement 'The Most Merciful established upon the Throne' (1), and al-yad in His statement 'The hand of Allah is above their hands' (2) and 'What prevented you from prostrating to what I created with My two hands?' (3), and al-wajh in His statement 'And there will remain the Face of your Lord' (4), and al-ayn in His statement 'That you be made under My eye' (0) and 'sailing under Our eyes' (1). So from the Shaykh [Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari?] that each of them is an additional attribute. And according to the majority—and it is one of the two sayings of the Shaykh—that they are metaphors: al-istiwa is a metaphor for domination or a representation and depiction of the majesty of Allah, al-yad is a metaphor for power, al-wajh for existence, and al-ayn for sight.
Source pages p. 174

Commentary
This passage addresses the so-called anthropomorphic verses (mutashābihāt) that appear in the Qur'an. The author states that the correct view is that terms like istiwā (establishing), yad (hand), wajh (face), and ʿayn (eye) are metaphors (majāzāt) and representations (tamthīlāt). He then gives verses as examples, noting that carrying them on their literal meanings is impossible (mumtaniʿ). He reports that according to al-Shaykh (probably Abū al-Ḥasan al-Ashʿarī), each of these terms could be considered a real attribute (ṣifa zāʾida), but the majority—and this is one of al-Shaykh's two opinions—hold that they are metaphors. Specifically, istiwā is a metaphor for istīlā' (domination) or a depiction of divine majesty; yad is a metaphor for qudra (power); wajh for wujūd (existence); and ʿayn for baṣar (sight).

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the Ash'ari school always affirms anthropomorphic attributes as real.
Correction: The passage shows that the majority view—and one of al-Ash'ari's own opinions—is that these terms are metaphors for divine attributes.

Checkpoint

B) It is a metaphor for power (القدرة)
C) It means a literal hand
D) It is unknowable

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari tradition interprets anthropomorphic Qur'anic expressions as metaphors for divine attributes—domination, power, existence, and sight—to uphold divine transcendence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
